It's about 125 acres. But here's the important part. For about 500 years, Clo de Vujo was run as a single property. The monks made the wine from different sections and then blended them together. Then came the French Revolution. In 1789, the state seized the church properties. Vineyards that monks had cultivated for centuries were auctioned off. Lo Vujo stayed intact for a while. The Uvar family owned it through most of the 1800s, but in 1889 it was sold to six wine merchants and then inheritance laws kicked in. The Napoleonic code was established in 1804 and it required that land be divided equally among all heirs. So generation after generation, these vineyards got subdivided and subdivided again. So today, Clo de Vujo has 80 different owners. Some own just a few rows of wines, and maybe it's enough to make a case or two of wine per year. And this is why Burgundy is so fragmented. And so when the French government created the official AOC classification in 1936, they went to Laval's work and they used that as the foundation. So it's 170 years later. - 10  Beloved Pawn Stars Members Who Tragically Passed Away: Kind: captions Language: en beloved members of a popular reality TV show who have tragically passed away. >> Do you still remember Pawn Stars? One of the most popular TV series that changed the narrative or perhaps restructured how you view reality shows. Have you ever wondered what happened to cast members who took a break and never returned to your screen? Think no more. Brace up and let's go on a ride as we reveal the mysteries surrounding Pawn Stars members that will leave you speechless. Richard Old Man Harrison. Richard Harrison, known as the old man, was one of the most beloved members of Pawn Stars. He had a serious face and a calm voice. Richard just gave the right advice to keep things in order. In the earlier days, he worked hard to grow the family business and wanted the shop to be strong and honest towards the customer moving forward. Every time he gave them advice or said no, he was really trying to help them. In 2018, Richard passed away at 77 years old. The news left fans heartbroken. But even after his death, the shop went on and the show kept going. But something felt different altogether. And when the viewers started watching the old episode him today, Mark Hall Patton. Mark Hall Patton was the historian with a big white beard, red glasses, and a voice filled with knowledge. Fans often referred to him as the beard of knowledge. On pawn stars, whenever someone brought in an old document or an antique with a long history behind it, Rick would immediately call in Mark. Mark wasn't just a guest on the show. He was a real museum expert. He ran museums in Las Vegas and knew more about history than most people around. He always made old things feel important. Mark wasn't funny or dramatic. Instead, he just loved sharing facts regarding those vintage items. Whether it was a Civil War letter or a rare old book, he looked at each one like it had a story to tell. But Mark's time on the show came to an end in a very unexpected way. In 2024, Mark Hall Patton had passed away due to a stroke. Many fans and collectors across the country shared their tributes. After his passing, the Las Vegas Museum honored him for his contributions. Fans remembered the way he could turn an old paper into a history lesson. But every time someone pulls a dusty book off a shelf or finds a handwritten letter in a box, it's time to call Mark to know more about it. Cory Harrison. Cory Harrison, also known as Big Hos, brought a young energy to the shop. He grew up in the pawn shop, watching the business closer each day. To most fans, he's always arguing with his dad, but he was actually day. He worked hard on making big deals and even pushed to use new technology in the show. In 14, Cory was faced with a brutal motorcycle accident. He was severely hurt and recovery was a slow process. In the same year, he got divorced care of himself. He still works in the shop, but the once funny and loving person was now transformed through pain. Rick Harrison Rishop. He was the son of Richard old man Harrison and the father of Corey. All the three generations worked together and Rick stood in the middle with his fast talk and deep knowledge of history. He was the one you saw the most. Rick made those deals and asked tough questions to keep the show moving that people trusted and also showcasing how history and hard work come together. But things don't always go according to the plan. After his father passed away, Rick felt the weight expected. His mother filed a lawsuit against him over the family's estate. It raised questions about trust within the family. Rick had to deal with his grief starts to fall apart. Rick Dale. Rick Dale was the go-to guy when something old and broken needed new life. Rick owned the shop called Rick's Restorations, and on Pawn Stars, he was known as the Restorer. Rick was a skilled craftsman. He could take a rusty gas pump or a beat up Coca-Cola machine and turn it into something beautiful. He was always patient and full of energy. Every time the pawn shop needed a repair, Rick would roll up in his truck, load the item, and bring it back with a whole different look. Rick's love for restoring old things made the viewers believe that history could be saved and not just studied. If something had value once, it could have value again. His business and family blended together. His wife and kids would help him at the shop, and it became more than just work. It was a way of life. But after several seasons of being a favorite on Pawn Stars and even getting his own spin-off show, American Restoration, Rick suddenly disappeared, so fans were left with questions. But Rick continued working in his shop, but without the cameras. The show changed, and fans missed the joy of watching him bring old things back to life. Chumley. Chumley, the most loved character of Pawn Stars, whose real name is Austin Russell, started working in the Pawn Shop long before the show began. He had been friends with Cory since they were kids. Fans loved how he didn't pretend to know everything. He asked the questions most of us would ask, but in 2016, everything changed. Chumley was arrested after the police searched his home and found harmful substances and weapons. It was a shocking situation and many thought his return to the show would be impossible. Chumley faced the consequences and worked hard to stay out of trouble. Eventually, he looked at his life and made a decision to change. He began eating healthier, working out, and lost over 100 lb of excessive weight. He reflected on the process of being better for himself and for the people who still believed in him. Interestingly, Chumley returned with his own candy shop in Las Vegas. Chumley's story reminds us that everyone makes these mistakes, but what truly matters is what you do next. He could have let shame take over, but instead he chose to grow, but while Chumbli found a new rhythm to life, another pawn stars favorite had his own reasons for pulling back. Steve Grad Steve Grad worked as an autograph expert. He could look at a signature and tell you if it was real or fake just by noticing small details. His job was to protect the shop from getting tricked and losing money. But over time, Steve began appearing less on the show. Eventually, he stopped showing up altogether due to a behind-the-scene conflict with the pawn shop. Fans noticed how much value he added to the show. His way of explaining things made complicated items easier to understand. He didn't try to be the center of attention. He let the facts speak for themselves. Steve Grad reminded us that even the smallest signature could carry a big story. Rebecca Romney. Rebecca was the rare book expert on the show. When someone brought in an old Bible, a first edition, or a dusty novel with strange markings, Rick looked up to Rebecca. She always arrived calm and collected and ready to unveil the story behind the book. But after a few seasons, her visits became less frequent. She chose to step away from the show entirely. Rebecca's time on Pawn Stars may have been limited, but it showcased that knowledge could be powerful. She made reading feel like treasure hunting, and she reminded us that sometimes the most valuable things aren't gold or silver, but the true stories hidden in those books. Brett Mey Brett Mey was the art expert. He worked at an art gallery and knew how to spot value in every frame. He helped people understand what made a painting special, not just how much it costs, but why it mattered. He wanted viewers to realize that art wasn't just for rich people or museums. But over time, Brett's visits became rare. Fans who appreciated his calm energy were left wondering. However, Brett brought balance to the show. And even though he didn't have a long goodbye, his presence made viewers feel calm and more curious. In the end, Brett didn't just appraise Art, he showed us how to appreciate it. And speaking of leaving, there was one more cast member whose exit was just as sudden. Olivia Black. Olivia Black joined the cast in season 5. She worked behind the counter guiding customers and learning the process. Olivia wanted to grow within the Pawn Shop. She was excited to be part of the show. It gave her a chance to prove herself. But not long after she started, news about her modeling career had been revealed. It wasn't a secret, but it didn't really fit the image of the show. Olivia disappeared from Pawn Stars and also expressed her grief due to the sudden exit. But instead of giving up, she chose to move forward and continue modeling. Her story reminds us that being different doesn't mean you don't belong. In a shop full of history, Olivia became part of it, too. And sometimes the hardest part of a story is the part that's never told on screen. Olivia's story is one of those. Over the years, Pawn Stars has brought all kinds of people through its doors, but each of them left something behind. And maybe that's what makes Pawn Stars more than just a show about old things. - Why isn’t the DR Congo the world’s richest country? | Mapped Out: Kind: captions Language: en-US Did you know that there’s probably a piece of this country in your smartphone? The Democratic Republic of the Congo, or DRC, could be one of the richest countries in the world. That’s because of what lies below the ground here. Vast resources, such as gold, cobalt and coltan. But the DRC is not rich. In fact, many Congolese people are among the world’s poorest. Congo is basically a resource-endowed country, but it is also a resource-cursed country. And it’s home to possibly the deadliest conflict since World War II. While people are distracted by the war, others are profiting from the natural resources. We’re going to look at how the DRC became a looting ground for the power-hungry and the corporate. And how even its neighbors have a hand in the exploitation. This is the Democratic Republic of the Congo. Also known as the DRC, or sometimes just the Congo. Not to be confused with its neighbor: the Republic of the Congo. The DRC is the largest country in Sub-Saharan Africa, and it’s bordered by nine countries. One of them, Rwanda, actually plays a big role in this tragedy. We’ll come back to that later. An estimated 110 million people live here in the DRC. 3 out of 4 of them often don’t have access to even the basics: Shelter, clean drinking water, and enough food. One in twelve children here dies before the age of 12. The DRC is among the five poorest countries in the world. It's this misery that drives hundreds of thousands of Congolese to work in mining sites like this one. And that’s where we want to start this story. This is dangerous work in dangerous conditions, and for just a few dollars a day. Child labor, threats and violence are often reported. Not to mention the vast environmental damage. All these things are happening because of the rush for riches under the ground. An estimated 24 trillion US dollars' worth of minerals lie beneath the surface. You have a country that is effectively one of the richest countries in the world. Certainly the wealthiest country in Africa, with all kinds of resources that you can imagine, some of which have not even been discovered. This is Paul Nantulya. He works at the Africa Center for Strategic Studies in Washington DC. You talk of any resource, name it, it is in the Congo, in plenty. For instance: Gold. For jewelry and electronics. Cobalt. Used for batteries, like in electric cars. The DRC has more than half of the world’s cobalt reserves. Copper: Vital for solar panels. And coltan. It’s literally everywhere in electronic devices, for example in your smartphone. An estimated 60 – 80% of global coltan reserves are in the DRC. So you can imagine, everybody wants a piece of this pie. Be they militia groups controlling informal mines... ...or huge multinational companies that have set up massive industrial complexes. This map shows some of the DRC’s major industrial cobalt, copper and gold mines. So who owns these mines? Well, the DRC has a minority share in many of them. But almost all are majority foreign-owned. Some by a Swiss company: Glencore. Then there are firms from Canada and the United Arab Emirates. But the most important player here is China. The red dots are majority Chinese-owned mines. Since the 1990s, China has been forging ties with the DRC, gaining more and more control over the DRC’s valuable natural resources. By the way: This huge mine was owned by a US company until the 2010s. But was sold off to a Chinese firm. A step the US government now regrets because it could REALLY use those resources to build electric cars. Chinese companies control 70% of the copper mining and 50% of the cobalt mining in the DRC. Let’s take this huge copper and cobalt mining site. It’s been majority Chinese-owned since the DRC and China struck a deal in 2008: the Sicomines agreement. The deal was about investment. In return for access to the mine, China pledged to build roads, schools and hospitals. Sounds like a win-win situation, right? But not much of the promised investment has materialized. Congolese people still have to make do with roads like THIS. So, where did all the money ACTUALLY go? Mostly lost in corruption. A key reason for poverty in the country. The DRC is ranked 162 out of 180 on Transparency International’s Corruption Perceptions Index. This basically means: corruption is everywhere in the country. Including in deals with multinational companies, whether European or Chinese. But it is not just outsiders to blame. The Congolese people have had governments thrusted upon them that are really of the worst type that one can ever imagine. So, it's basically the DRC’s leaders who have profited from the resources. And we’re about to meet them. When it comes to the DRC’s long history of exploitation, we kind of have to start with this man. King Leopold II of Belgium. In 1885, he seized this territory, as his personal property. And named it "Congo Free State." It was anything but. An estimated 10 million Congolese were killed, or died from hunger or disease, during Leopold’s cruel rule. Many were enslaved and forced to work, collecting rubber, used for making car and bicycle tires. And the exploitation continued after 1908, when Belgium turned the "Congo Free State" into a colony. But in 1960, there was hope for a new start. This was the year the DRC gained independence, and its first prime minister: Patrice Lumumba. Even today, many in the DRC see him as a national hero. Lumumba had a vision for a just nation. The country’s natural resources should be used for the benefit of its citizens. But just months later, Lumumba was captured by his opponents, and murdered. with the tacit backing of Belgium and the US. They wanted him out of the way, fearing he was getting too close to the Soviet Union during the Cold War. So, the Congo has never recovered from that tragedy. And I think this is the first reason why the country is in repeated cycles of trauma and crisis. One of the issues that has constantly plagued the Democratic Republic of Congo has been political instability and chronic mismanagement by successive governments. This is Beverly Ochieng speaking. A security expert based in Dakar, Senegal. And when she says governments... there haven’t actually been many. That’s because dictator Joseph Desiré Mobutu ruled for 32 years. Mobutu, with his distinctive leopard print hat, renamed the country: "Zaire." And he grew eye-wateringly rich, looting the national coffers at the people’s expense. Western countries, including the US, were willing to turn a blind eye. They wanted to keep him close to them, and away from the Soviet Union. Because: you guessed it. They, of course, wanted priority access to the country’s resources. Once, the US even sent Mobutu several planes, in a bid to secure mining rights. He demanded that one of them be loaded with $60,000 USD worth of Coca-Cola. The kleptocratic rule, that was refined and perfected by Mobutu, unfortunately, it became part and parcel of how the country is governed. And so, it continued, long after Mobutu. First under Laurent Kabila, then under his son Joseph, who was notoriously corrupt. Again, the DRC’s mineral wealth was sold off to foreign investors, and at laughably low prices. When current president Felix Tshisekedi took office in 2019, he pledged to crack down on corruption. But both his elections were marred by fraud allegations. And for many people, not enough has changed. I think there have been some small strides. Whether that government has actually met some of the campaign objectives that they had put in place, is another question. So, we’ve seen how corruption and exploitation stand in the way of a brighter future for Congolese people. But there’s another major hurdle. The biggest reason for the poverty in the Congo, especially in the eastern part of the Democratic Republic of Congo, is due to the war. This is Ruth Alonga, a DW correspondent in Goma. The conflict she is talking about is happening in her region, in the east of the DRC. More than 120 different militias are active here. Yes, you’ve heard right: one hundred and twenty. Some of them are small, local groups. Others are larger militias with foreign backing, like the M23 and the FDLR. We’ll come back to them. These militias have different aims and agendas. And one way of financing those goals? Exactly. We’re back to the minerals. Look at the provinces worst hit by the conflict: Ituri, North Kivu and South Kivu. That’s also where many of the DRC’s valuable natural resources are found. So, it's a conflict belt, but it's also a resource belt. The minerals themselves do end up feeding into the conflict because then armed groups can take territories, make them their own, and run an entire operation there. Which then undermines the possibility of them being used for the betterment of the community. Let’s take this example: In 2024, M23 militants took over these mining sites near the town of Rubaya. This area is where most of the DRC’s coltan comes from. Remember that one? It’s one of the minerals needed in your smartphone. So: It’s valuable. And lucrative for any groups that can get their hands on it. But there’s much more to this conflict. The minerals have probably added to it, but the fact that there's been ethnic animosities which go way back to the colonial period are not necessarily about the minerals themselves. Those just fuel the situation, but are not the underlying cause. To unpack that, we need to go back in time. And head to one of the DRC’s neighbors: Rwanda. Where, in 1994, a brutal genocide was unfolding. One ethnic group had turned against another. The Hutu majority against the ruling Tutsi minority. In the end, extremist Hutus killed around 800,000 Tutsis and moderate Hutus. During that time, many Rwandans fled across the border into the DRC. Among them: Some of the Hutu perpetrators. The aftermath of the Rwandan genocide still influences the conflict in eastern DRC today. And the blame game that surrounds it. Two of the biggest militias are proof of that: The FDLR started with those Hutu extremists linked to the genocide. Then there’s the M23. Its members are mainly ethnic Tutsis, so the victims of the genocide. The DRC accuses Rwanda of supporting the M23. And the other way round: Rwanda says the DRC is working with the FDLR. Both governments deny that. It’s messy, but bear with us. There is another key group: the ADF. It’s very difficult to find pictures of them... ...just of what they leave behind: a trail of destruction. The Islamist militia originated in Uganda, and has ties to the so-called Islamic State. Decades of fighting by these groups and many others have left around 6 million people dead since 1996. Today, a further 7 million people in the DRC are internally displaced, the UN reports. Those people have lost everything. The Congolese army is supposed to contain the conflict. But they, like it seems everyone else, have faced allegations of human rights abuses and corruption. And maintaining an army is costly. The war also absorbed financial resources that could be used for maybe developing infrastructure, education and healthcare, and so on. So, many people keep on suffering, while others profit. Congo has had neighbors that have not been good neighbors, neighbors that have exploited Congo's governance gaps. There is no doubt that Rwanda benefits massively from the instability in the DRC. Most of the gold Rwanda exports is believed to be Congolese gold, smuggled across the border. And while the DRC is the world’s biggest coltan PRODUCER, the biggest coltan EXPORTER is Rwanda. But despite the smuggling, the European Union struck a controversial mineral deal with Rwanda. Local NGOs say this deal even fuels the conflicts. Rwanda is not the only place these resources get to. UN experts found gold and other minerals from the DRC were also being smuggled to Uganda, Burundi, Tanzania, and even the United Arab Emirates. From there, you know, they make their way into international markets, or into the international black market. And again, possibly into your smartphone. So where are we at? International efforts to tackle these problems haven’t worked. Ceasefire agreements have broken into pieces. The UN’s biggest-ever peacekeeping mission has largely failed. The fact that many of the initiatives that are trying, that are being driven, to ensure a level of stability, or a level of political goodwill, keep on coming undone, each and every time, means that there are fairly bleak prospects for DR Congo. So, is there at least a little hope for the Congo? Congo needs a democratic, legitimate government that is respected and given legitimacy by the people. To hope for a better future, a bright future, the government has to involve the local population in managing these resources. That seems like wishful thinking for now. One of the longest- running conflicts on Earth looks set to continue. And others won’t stop profiting from the country’s riches.
