- The Endless War For DR Congo: Kind: captions Language: en These maps show all the mines currently being extracted in the eastern region of the Democratic Republic of Congo. There are approximately 2,951 mines employing 427,469 artisanal miners in this region alone in the South Kivu province. There are more than 450 mining companies. Unfortunately, most of them are operating illegally. Operations that ultimately result in violence such as this. The DRC is home to trillions of dollars worth of untapped minerals, but has also been the victim of persistent conflict since independence. This conflict plays out mostly in the eastern region, which has become home to over a hundred armed groups amidst competition for control of mines and trade routes. As a result, what should be a blessing of mineral wealth has instead financed the warlords, displaced millions of people, and created one of the worst humanitarian crisis in the world. But, why is the Democratic Republic of Congo, which is the second largest country in Africa, unable to control its mineral wealth with different armed groups and powerful countries scrambling to tear it apart all while distributing its minerals amongst themselves? I want to show you how mineral operations fuel conflict in this African country. We will look at the lucrative minerals at stake, the key actors involved including their international sponsors, the underground network they use to get funding for their operations, and the wide-ranging impacts of these activities on the people of Congo. This is the story of the Democratic Republic of Congo and why everyone wants a piece of it. The mining potential of this country is just beyond imaginable, and yet you have poverty at the highest level. It all began in 1960. The Democratic Republic of Congo had just gotten its independence from Belgium, but not everyone was happy about this. The new Prime Minister, Patrice Lumumba, had sent a strong message to imperialists during his speech at the Independence Day celebration. A speech that left the King of Belgium fuming. Lumumba wanted independence to mean something for his people. He wanted a legitimate improvement in the quality of life for the Congolese and to put an end to tribalism. His first act to achieve his goals was to replace the white man in his administration with locals. He wanted the colonial masters gone. The Belgians, of course, didn't like this, and they made it their mission to fight Lumumba every step of the way. You see, if Lumumba had succeeded, the Belgians would have lost access to lots of mineral resources in this region called Katanga province. Their businesses, mines, and whole industries were located here. If those had closed down, it would have resulted in a huge unemployment crisis for the Belgian government back in Brussels. So, they blocked this move. Over in Washington, D.C., the CIA were monitoring the situation in the DRC. According to these powerful forces, Lumumba was beginning to sound and act like communists back in China and the Soviet Union. So, they launched a secret operation to stop him. You see, the new government of the DRC that was formed at independence was headed by this guy, Kasa-Vubu, who was serving as president while Lumumba was serving as prime minister. But, shortly after the Independence Day celebration and the festivities that followed, trouble began. Many soldiers hoped for independence to result in immediate promotions and better pay, but were left frustrated by the continual presence of the Belgians. This soon led to widespread mutinies across the country. Lumumba took steps to halt the crisis by replacing Belgian soldiers in the army leadership with Congolese men and ordering some of the Belgians to leave. Yet, they refused. One of the promotions he made was Joseph Mobutu as colonel and army chief of staff, a decision he would later come to regret. Unknown to Lumumba, Mobutu had strong ties to Belgian and US intelligence services, ties that would later be exploited by the CIA. All European officers in the army were replaced with Africans with a few retained as advisors. Despite all of these efforts, the mutinies continued to spread throughout the entire country. At this point, Belgium felt it had seen enough and decided to get involved. On the 10th of July, 1960, barely 10 days after independence, Belgium sent 6,000 troops to the Congo to protect its citizens from the violence. You see, most Europeans had gone to Katanga province, which has most of Congo's natural resources. So, the Belgian troops, which were sent to protect Belgian citizens, landed mainly in the same province of Katanga. Unknown to Lumumba, however, the Belgians had other ideas. This guy, Moise Tshombe, was a political opponent of Lumumba. At the time of this crisis, he was overseeing the affairs of the resource-rich Katanga province under Lumumba's government. Behind the scenes, however, he was working for the Belgians. A day after the arrival of the Belgian soldiers, Tshombe, backed by the foreign soldiers from Belgium, declared that the mineral-rich province of Katanga was seceding from the Congo to form its own country. This led to chaos in the new nation. As the crisis wore on, another region known as South Kasai was threatening to follow in Katanga's footsteps and declare independence from the DRC. This newly independent country was now on the verge of collapse. And this is when the United States decided to get involved. The CIA deployed its new head of station, Chief Larry Devlin, into the DRC on the 10th of July, 1960, to monitor situations on the ground. While working closely with US Ambassador Clayton Blair, Devlin took just a few days to come to the conclusion that Prime Minister Patrice Lumumba was a dangerous man and he needed to go. With his mission decided, Devlin started to secretly recruit some of the most prominent Congolese political leaders known for their hatred of Lumumba. Back in the DRC capital, Lumumba was working hard at getting the Belgians to leave. He had protested the presence of the Belgian forces to the UN and even requested that they be replaced with UN forces since they claimed to be there on a peacekeeping mission. But, no matter what he did, the UN kept blocking his attempts to expel the Belgian troops and defeat the Katanga rebels. He made a plea to the US to intervene. Unfortunately, though, the US is one and the same with the UN. They too turned him down. To Lumumba, the West were never going to act against one of their own. In this frustration, he sent a telegram to Soviet Premier Nikita Khrushchev requesting that he closely monitor the situation in the Congo and provide some assistance in dealing with the secessionist movement in the province of Katanga. This move immediately made the Americans more obsessed than ever with the prime minister. As usual, the CIA launched a propaganda campaign against Lumumba to discredit him. The CIA claimed that Lumumba's entourage was full of KGB agents. Bear in mind that this was deep in the middle of the Cold War, and the Americans would stop at nothing to get ahead of the Soviet Union. In fact, Ambassador Timberlake was able to influence Henry Luce, Time magazine's owner, to stop the publication of a cover story about Lumumba all in the name of America's interest. Same month, in a wire message to CIA headquarters, Devlin wrote, quote, "Patrice Lumumba was born to be a revolutionary, but he doesn't have the qualities to exercise power once he seized it. Sooner or later, Moscow will take the reins. He believes he can manipulate the Soviets, but they are the ones pulling the strings." End of quote. On the 26th of August, 1960, Allen Dulles, the director of the CIA, replied saying, quote, "If Lumumba continues to be in power, the result will be at best chaos and at worst an eventual seizure of power by the for the prestige of the UN and the interests of the free world. His dismissal must therefore be an urgent and a priority objective for you." End of quote. As soon as both parties agreed on what should be done, a budget of $100,000 was released to the CIA station chief, who organized anti-Lumumba demonstrations that led to serious violence. While the chaos on the streets tried to discredit the work of Lumumba, the United States kept pressuring the DRC's president, Kasa-Vubu, to remove and replace his prime minister. On the 5th of September, 1960, this pressure worked. Kasa-Vubu dismissed Lumumba and replaced him with this guy, Joseph Ileo. However, Lumumba decided to fight back. He refused to leave his post and won parliamentary backing. This soon resulted in a constitutional crisis for the new nation. Realizing that this path has been blocked, the CIA turned to other means. Remember I said thing Joseph Mobutu, who was made colonel and army chief of staff by Lumumba? Yeah, he reappeared just when the CIA needed him to complete the task. In Washington, Mobutu was considered temperate, competent, and pro-Western. So, one evening in early September, 1960, Devlin had a meeting with Kasa-Vubu at the president's house. While he was waiting in the living room, Mobutu appeared and suggested to him that he had gathered some commanders who were ready to overthrow Lumumba and that he needed the support of the United States. The CIA, without hesitation, agreed to this plan. They granted Mobutu's request of $5,000 US for his officers and the plan was a go. A few days later, on the 14th of September 1960, Mobutu seized power for the first time. Lumumba was arrested and a civilian government installed. All diplomatic relations with the Soviet Union and China ended with immediate effect. This marked the beginning of the end to the Democratic Republic of Congo. Few months after this coup, Lumumba was assassinated. But, the DRC government didn't witness any progress after the removal of Lumumba. Rather, when this guy, Joseph Mobutu, also known as Mobutu Sese Seko, seized power, he plundered the wealth of the DRC all with the backing of the United States and his Western allies, mostly Belgium. Yeah, all that mutiny and unrest in the Katanga province that led to the Congo crisis of 1960 were all orchestrated by the Belgian government to destabilize and gain control over the mineral wealth of the DRC. But, what is this mineral wealth you keep hearing about? You see, if there ever was a land so blessed, yet so cursed, it would be the Democratic Republic of Congo. This country is said to hold an estimated $24 trillion US in untapped minerals. On paper, it's one of the richest countries in the world in terms of natural resources. The DRC holds a large deposit of tin, tantalum, tungsten, and gold. These four minerals are commonly referred to as conflict minerals as they are generally mined in this area of armed conflict and traded illicitly to finance armed operations. These minerals are essential to global industries, which is why they are at the center of the violence that continues to devastate this region. There is a network of warlords, militias, and corrupt officials who facilitate the cycle of this conflict. And since the global demand minerals continues to rise, the illegal trade continues to grow despite efforts to regulate it. Some other key minerals found in the DRC include cobalt. An estimated 70 to 75% of all global supply of cobalt is found deposited in the DRC. Yeah, cobalt is used in lithium-ion batteries that power our laptop, smartphones, and even electric vehicles. According to Statista, the DRC has over 6 million metric tons of cobalt. And then there's lithium, which is another essential mineral found in large quantities in the DRC. It's also used in lithium-ion batteries for laptops, smartphones, and electric vehicles. The DRC is also rich in gold with some estimates saying the country holds about 2% of the world's total supply of gold. Gold is used in jewelry, electronics, and as a financial asset. There's also coltan, which is used in solder for circuit boards in electronics. The DRC is said to have around 40 to 60% of the world's supply of coltan. The DRC has approximately 10% of global supply of copper, which is an essential material for electric infrastructures, wiring, and renewable energy systems. The DRC is also blessed with about 20% of the global supply of industrial grade diamonds used for cutting tools, drilling, and high-tech applications. These minerals combined are valued at around $24 trillion. But, where are they located in the DRC? This will give you an insight into why the fighting in this country is happening right here in these two regions. A large amount of the DRC's mineral wealth is found in the eastern and southern regions, particularly in the provinces of Katanga, which is rich in copper and cobalt. North and South Kivu is rich in coltan, gold, tin, and tungsten. Ituri has significant gold deposits. Maniema is rich in tin, tungsten, and coltan. Kasai has large diamond deposits and home to all diamond operations. Here's an interactive map by the International Peace Information Service showing the mineral-rich areas in the DRC. These minerals are extremely valuable because they feed global industries from consumer electronics to electric car batteries to fine jewelry and financial reserves. Now, the skyrocketing international demand and high prices create an incentive for anyone with a gun in eastern DRC to try to get a piece of the trade. As a result, what should be a bedrock of development for Congo instead bankrolls conflict and exploitation. But, hang on a second. Why eastern DRC? How did this place become the epicenter of conflict minerals? Now, let's get into that story. According to the US Geological Survey, the DRC's high-value minerals are easily extractable since they are often found in shallow deposits accessible to us in a miners. This makes the DRC a magnet for opportunists. Take for example, neighboring Rwanda and Uganda have relatively modest mineral resources. Yet, Rwanda has miraculously become the world's largest official exporter of coltan in recent years. An obvious sign that Congolese ore is being rerouted through its territory. In short, the DRC's mountains are a treasure trove that has drawn in predators from near and far. The abundance is such that even basic mining methods can yield valuable ore, which is why suspicious actors from local villagers to foreign armies have tried to stake a claim. But, how did it get this bad that we now have so many armed groups, foreign governments, and militias fighting for resources in a sovereign country? Well, to answer this question, we need to travel all the way back to the 1990s. But, let me pause here for a minute. I need to give you an important piece of information that will be relevant to our story shortly. You see, the conflict you keep hearing about in the DRC is majorly in this eastern part of the country. Coincidentally, this part consists of the provinces known for significant mineral wealth, namely North Kivu, South Kivu, Ituri, and northern Katanga. Interestingly, this eastern part of the DRC shares borders with countries like Uganda, Rwanda, Burundi, and Tanzania. This is important to note. Now, let's hop back in that time machine. The year is 1994. The world is still trying to come to terms with the brutal genocide unfolding in Rwanda. By the time Paul Kagame and his rebel forces finally break through and overthrows the Rwandan government, around 2 million refugees, including members of the militias responsible for this genocide, had gained entry into eastern Congo, then known as Zaire, mostly settling in refugee camps in the North Kivu and South Kivu provinces. A small group of those Rwandans who entered DRC are Hutu extremists who began organizing militias within this eastern Congo region. They are bitter about the war they just lost to Paul Kagame and his Tutsi rebels. Pressure soon intensifies as Tutsi militias organize themselves against the Hutu groups. Soon enough, foreign powers with vested interests begin to get involved. This forms the backdrop of the crisis and violence that would come to haunt this region of the DRC. As time passes, ethnic tension within the region continues to grow. Reports of violent clashes between militias, local communities, and refugee groups soon surface. This endless violence results in local infrastructures collapsing. Refugees and locals are now witnessing increasing hunger and diseases. People are becoming desperate looking for something, anything to do to protect themselves and their families. Then, came the rising demand for mobile phones. As technological advancement gives rise to a surge in mobile phone production from around 60 million units to about 250 million units, demand for minerals like coltan sees a dramatic surge swelling violence in this eastern region of the DRC. Armed groups began seizing mines to fund their operations. Back in Rwanda, Kagame is getting reports of the activities of these Hutu extremists whom he fears could decide to launch an invasion into his country. So, he comes up with a plan to stop it. But, he needs a face for this plan, a local, someone with ties to the DRC. Enters this rebel leader, Laurent Désiré Kabila, who forms the Alliance of Democratic Forces for the Liberation of Congo-Zaire with the goal of overthrowing Mobutu Sese Seko. This rebel group soon becomes a coalition of Rwandan, Ugandan, Burundian, and Congolese dissidents. So, with the plan in place, Rwandan troops and Congo-based Tutsi militias with Rwandan backing join forces with Laurent Désiré Kabila to launch an invasion of Zaire. This marks the beginning of the First Congo War. Kigali justifies its efforts by arguing that Hutu groups in eastern DRC were still a threat to their Tutsi population and that the Mobutu regime was harboring Hutu extremists who had fled across the border. But, remember that Paul Kagame wasn't fighting this war alone. Rwanda waged the First Congo War against Zaire with the help of other African states, most significantly Uganda, but also Angola and Burundi, who had their own security concerns related to Mobutu's support of rebel groups across the continent. When this war ended, millions of people had been displaced with large-scale looting and destruction of properties and infrastructure. Those that survived were left to deal with diseases and malnutrition that killed tens of thousands more. For a brief moment, all the Congolese people had to deal with was diseases and malnutrition until another war broke out. This time, in 1998, following the breakdown of relationship between Paul Kagame and Laurent Désiré Kabila. There are reports the Congolese army has teamed up When this alliance broke down, Kabila ordered all foreign troops out of the Congo and allowed Hutu armed groups to organize at the border once again. Paul Kagame responded by invading in 1998. By 1999, both Uganda and Rwanda had armed groups fighting in the DRC. And here is where it gets interesting. The reason Paul Kagame gave for this Second Congo War was to create a zone in the DRC-Rwanda borderlands controlled by its own troops to create more distance from Hutu groups in eastern DRC. Remember this eastern region of DRC with all of these mineral resources? Yeah, that's the one. Soon enough, this conflict exploded into a regional war. On the one side were Congolese forces supported by Angola, Namibia, and Zimbabwe. The Rwandan side had Uganda and Burundian militaries, as well as various rebel groups supported by Kigali and Kampala. In July of 1999, a year after this brutal fighting began, the Lusaka Peace Agreement was signed by heads of state from Angola, Democratic Republic of the Congo, Namibia, Rwanda, Uganda, and Zimbabwe. It called for a ceasefire, deployment of a UN peacekeeping force, and the release of prisoners of war. This agreement also created a framework for a power-sharing government and led to the withdrawal of foreign troops. Major rebel groups, including the Rwandan-backed RCD and the Ugandan-backed MLC, were granted representation alongside the existing Congolese leadership and political opposition. However, this agreement failed to bring an end to the wider conflict. The country remained divided with armed groups controlling large parts of the mineral-rich region. By 2001, President Laurent Désiré Kabila was assassinated by one of his bodyguards. His son, Joseph Kabila, assumed power amid the ongoing conflict. But, the death of Laurent Kabila only prolonged the devastating conflict further, with the country now destabilized even more. In 2002, the key actors in this conflict were assembled by mediators in South Africa, where the Sun City Agreement was signed. This agreement established a transitional government that included the president, Joseph Kabila, representatives from different rebel factions, political oppositions, and civil society groups. But, there was just this one problem with this arrangement. While the basic framework for this power-sharing largely worked and laid the foundation for national unification and a democratic transition, the decision to hand power to factions with a history of violence and exploitation only increased instability within the country. Rebel groups continued to control large mineral-rich parts of the country, particularly in this eastern region. This meant all the resources being extracted were illegally smuggled out of the country without any benefit to the people of the DRC. Profits from this illegal trade made these rebel groups stronger, as they were now able to buy more sophisticated weapons from the global market. In 2006, the DRC held its first democratic election. Joseph Kabila won this election, which happened to be the first in 40 years. However, while the president and his circle celebrated this win, armed groups continued to rule over mineral-rich regions of the country, with large recruitment of child soldiers reported. In January 2008, the International Rescue Committee reported that an estimated 5.4 million people had died over the 10-year period of this conflict, nearly half of them children under five. Most importantly, this year also saw a major turn of events in the war. This is Laurent Nkunda, a major rebel leader. In 2008, he and his CNDP rebel faction were integrated into the Congolese army, marking a major twist in the saga. But, this integration didn't happen as the group had expected. Soon enough, the dissatisfaction that arose led to the creation of this other group called the M23, a rebel group backed by the Rwandan government. Activities of this M23 group led to the displacement of tens of thousands of civilians. The city of Goma, in particular, endured brutal violence. But, the worst was yet to come. Over in the United States, a new technology was about to hit the market. The launch of the smartphone would make life even more complicated for Congolese people. You see, the smartphone gave rise to a surge in global demand for minerals like tantalum, tin, tungsten, and gold. When these armed fighters in the DRC learned of this new device, they further tightened their grip on these mineral-rich areas. Groups that were considering laying down their arms before now had a new reason to fight on. Control over these mining operations soon made some of them millionaires. With the profits, they bought more arms, recruited more hands, and the battle continued. The US government, seeing what was happening, tried to intervene. US lawmakers soon passed the Dodd-Frank Act, which mandates the disclosure of conflict minerals sourced from the DRC and surrounding regions. This law aimed to cut off funding to armed groups profiting from the mineral trade. However, it sort of created a new loophole for these armed groups to exploit. The market suddenly went underground. Smuggling networks were soon established, further complicating the supply chains for legitimate businesses. These groups that were only making money from the exports of conflict minerals now had a new revenue stream in smuggling operations. Today, 20% of global supply of tantalum, tin, and tungsten originates from the DRC, much of it tied to conflict zones. In November 2012, the M23 rebel group seized the city of Goma, displacing approximately 140,000 people. This rebel group held the city for about 2 weeks, during which time they controlled key strategic locations and continued to disrupt humanitarian aid efforts. The United States and Congolese forces, with support from the UN's Intervention Brigade, launched a coordinated military response to drive M23 out of the city. By late November 2012, M23 was forced to retreat and ultimately went into exile in Rwanda and Uganda. However, while the UN and other international coalition continued to fight off militia groups pillaging this eastern region of the DRC, the central government was contributing to the violence. By 2017, another crisis was brewing in the DRC. President Joseph Kabila, whose time in office had expired, refused to step down. This led to protests and a political crisis, giving room to armed militias who took advantage of the situation. Then came the push for EVs and renewable energy technology, further increasing demand for Congolese cobalt and coltan, resulting in more violence and In 2018, Félix Tshisekedi became the first president in Congo's history to take power through a peaceful transition. Though the legitimacy of his victory was widely questioned. But, while the election brought hope for peace, fighting continued in the east. M23 resurfaced yet again, this time capturing key towns in North Kivu. Investigators soon began digging into the funding of this group, and here was this tiny country to the east, Rwanda. The reports allege that the group receives military support, including weapons and trainings, from Rwanda, despite international condemnation. The M23 continues to operate in the region. Reports confirm the group's continued access to arms and cross-border support, undermining peace efforts in the process. A UN Group of Experts reported that M23's control over Goma and surrounding areas provides them access to significant mineral wealth, including coltan and cassiterite mines. The exploitation and smuggling of these conflict minerals fund the rebel group's activities and fuel regional instability. In 2025, M23 rebels, reportedly backed by up to 4,000 Rwandan troops, seized Goma and Bukavu, the largest cities in eastern DRC, marking a significant escalation in the conflict. Following the capture of Bukavu, M23 forces advanced south towards Uvira. Remember, this region of the country is home to the majority of the mineral deposits we talked about earlier. And now, a large portion of it is in the hands of M23, a rebel group with support from a hostile country. But, hold on now. These guys are not the only groups with illegal activities in the DRC. There are dozens, if not hundreds, of other actors profiting from the DRC's conflict mineral economy. There are generally four key actors in the DRC's conflict mineral economy. Some are formal, while others are mostly informal and carry out illegal operations in the dark. We have state-sanctioned mining companies. The DRC's formal mining sector includes state-owned enterprises and multinational corporations operating under government licenses. In the copper and cobalt rich southeast, mostly the former Katanga region, companies like Glencore, China Molybdenum, and others operate huge industrial mines in partnership with DRC state mining firm Gécamines. In the gold sector, ventures such as Kibali Gold Mine, jointly run by Barrick Gold and AngloGold Ashanti, with state company Sokimo, produce tons of gold per year under legal concession. These state sanctioned operations generate billions in export value. Yet, they are largely isolated in pockets of relative stability. In eastern provinces like North and South Kivu and Ituri, industrial mining is limited. Although a few companies like Canada's Alphamin at the Bisie tin mine or Chinese joint ventures with state miner Sakima in Kivu exist, the vast majority in DRC is artisanal and dominated by informal actors. And when we get into illegal mining, there are surprising elements operating here. Chief among them is the national army and officials. Ironically, personnels of the Congolese national army are also complicit in illegal mining. Units of the Congolese army have frequently been reported to interfere in mining areas, extort miners, or run illicit roadblocks for tax in eastern DRC. In some cases, army commanders form secret arrangements with militias to share profits instead of fighting them. For example, UN investigators have documented Congolese national army collaborating with FDLR rebels to smuggle resources like charcoal and protect illicit gold trading networks involving neighboring countries. Corrupt officials and local strongmen further enable the black market economy by granting fake permits or accepting bribes to turn a blind eye. This has resulted in the formation of a hybrid economy in which armed groups, rogue soldiers, and shadowy business networks all work together to loot the resources of the DRC. The third key actors are the armed groups and militias. There are more than 120 different armed groups active in the eastern Democratic Republic of Congo. These include well-known factions such as the March 23 Movement we talked about earlier. And then we have the Democratic Forces for the Liberation of Rwanda, FDLR. There's the Allied Democratic Forces, ADF. There's Codeco militias in Ituri and numerous Mai-Mai local self-defense groups. Many of these groups actively exploit minerals. For example, the M23 rebellion seized the strategic town of Rubaya in 2024, an area that produces roughly 15% of the world's coltan and has since enjoyed a lucrative income by controlling mining and trade there. Militias set up their own taxation system at mine sites and along transport routes using intimidation or force to compel miners and traders to pay. Even political or ethnic-based groups often end up behaving like criminal rackets funding themselves through this illicit resource extraction. Gold trade is one major source of financing for these armed groups in the DRC. Most of the trade goes unrecorded and most of the transactions are concluded in neighboring cities such as Kampala, the Ugandan capital, the Kenyan capital city of Nairobi, Bujumbura, the economic capital of Burundi, as well as the northern Tanzanian town of Mwanza. In addition to selling real gold, criminal networks also organize elaborate scams to sell counterfeit gold to international clients. This is according to a report by the UN Security Council. The last of these key actors are international players. You see, the conflict mineral trade extends far beyond DRC's borders. During the Congo Wars, the Rwandan and Ugandan national armies became major players by occupying mining zones and directly exporting resources. Today, Rwanda and Uganda no longer openly station troops in DRC, but they exert influence through proxies and economic networks. Rwanda in particular is accused of backing the M23 rebellion as a means to secure access to minerals. Rwandan officials deny this, but evidence, including UN reports, indicates Kigali supports M23 military while benefiting financially from Congolese minerals smuggled into Rwanda. The UN has long maintained its position that the Rwandan army is in de facto to control of M23 operations, detailing how M23 recruits are trained under Rwandan supervision and supported by high-tech Rwandan weaponry. However, President Kagame has repeatedly denied any involvement in supporting the M23 rebels, who are well-equipped, well-armed, and well-trained. According to the UN's 293-page report, Uganda is also part of the sponsors of these armed groups operating in the DRC. The UN report said it was likely that Uganda was allowing M23 supplies and new recruits through its territory. Ugandan military intelligence officers had also been in the Congolese town of Bunangana since at least late 2023 to coordinate with M23 leaders, provide logistics, and transport M23 leaders to M23 controlled areas. The report also noted that M23 leaders, including its military chief Sultani Makenga, who is subject to a UN sanctions travel ban, had traveled to Uganda for meetings. Another international player here is Burundi, who has also continued to meddle, though on a smaller scale. Burundian rebels and army units have crossed into South Kivu and Burundi is soil is sometimes used as a smuggling route for Congolese gold as Burundi has minimal production of its own. Tanzania and Kenya play roles mainly as transit routes. Congolese minerals often exit Africa via Dar es Salaam or Mombasa ports after being laundered through adjacent countries. Angola, historically, was interested in Congolese diamonds and oil along the western border. Zimbabwe is also part of this mix. During the Second Congo War, Zimbabwe sent troops to support the DRC government and was rewarded with access to mining concessions. Zimbabwean military companies got diamond and cobalt rights, for example. So, the Congo Wars drew in a constellation of African nations, all seeking a share of the riches. The lingering outcome is that regional elites in several countries continue to benefit from Congo's illicit mineral trade, creating a vested interest in keeping the crisis ongoing. And how can we talk about international players without mentioning the super powers? In the past 10 to 15 years, China has become the most significant global economic player in the DRC's mining sector. Although the Chinese approach has been largely financial and diplomatic, not military. China's motivations are clear, secure long-term access to strategic minerals like cobalt, copper, and increasingly lithium for its industries. This has led to Chinese state-owned companies and private firms investing billions of dollars in Congolese mining. Notably, in 2007, the DRC struck a huge deal with Chinese companies, often called the minerals-for-infrastructure Sicomines deal. Terms of this deal stated that Chinese firms would build roads, railways, and hospitals in DRC in exchange for copper and cobalt concessions. Today, Chinese companies either own or finance a majority of DRC's large copper-cobalt mines. An example is this China Molybdenum, which owns the giant Tenke Fungurume mine. Zijin Mining Co. owns the Kamoya-Kakula copper project. By some estimates, as of the mid-2020s, China controls 15 of the 19 largest cobalt mines in DRC, directly or through partnerships. But western powers are gradually catching on. Western involvement in DRC's minerals has evolved over time. In the late 19th and 20th century, it was colonial exploitation by Belgium's King Leopold II and companies like Forminière and UMHK. Post-independence, western Cold War interests led to CIA involvement in coups like the assassination of Lumumba in 1961 that we talked about earlier. This had a strategic resource angle as Katanga's secession was supported by Belgium and other foreign mining interests. From the 1990s onward, western governments have publicly advocated for stability and human rights in DRC, but western corporations have often been more pragmatic. In the early 2000s, a UN panel of experts report named dozens of companies from Europe and North America that were allegedly buying blood minerals from Congo's war zones, including tantalum processors and gold dealers who sourced from rebel-held mines. This led to some reputational damage and pressure on those companies. By the 2010s, western tech firms like Apple, Intel, Tesla, etc. came under increasing pressure from consumers and activists to ensure conflict-free minerals in their products. This in turn pressured their suppliers, smelters, and component manufacturers to verify sources. Laws like Dodd-Frank in the US pushed this agenda. However, loopholes remain. Gold in particular has been identified as entering western markets via ghost states without proper checks, and cobalt, which was conveniently not covered by the conflict mineral rules, continued to flow from Congo to major electronics and EV companies, often with documented child labor and human rights issues. Western mining corporations also directly operate in DRC like Barrick Gold, Glencore, Ivanhoe Mines with billionaire Robert Friedland. These companies have found a way to negotiate sweet deals with the weak government of Meanwhile, there are also individual players like Alain Goetz, a Belgian gold dealer who operated the African gold refinery in Uganda. This man has created transnational smuggling enterprises that he and his networks used to plunder the wealth of the DRC. US Treasury sanctions revealed that Goetz's network was moving hundreds of millions of dollars worth of illicit DRC gold through Uganda and onto global markets. In the process, providing the largest source of revenue to armed groups in eastern DRC. These external actors, including companies in Dubai, traders in Europe, and regional business people form the back end of the conflict supply chain, laundering resources and profits out of Congo. Now, to understand why this trade is turning into a do-or-die affair for these actors, we need to look at the scale and profitability of mining in the DRC. Just how lucrative is the Congo's illicit mining economy? Mining is by far the biggest sector of the DRC's economy. It is the engine behind both legitimate GDP and the shadow economy that forms conflict. To appreciate the scale, let's compare the legal mining and the illegal or semi-legal mining in economic terms. In the formal mining sector, the DRC is dominated by large projects in copper, cobalt, and industrial diamonds, mostly located in the south and parts of the east. This sector makes up the vast majority of DRC's export earnings, an estimated 90% plus, and a significant chunk of government revenue through taxes and royalties, though exact figures vary by year. For example, in 2021, the value of DRC's mining exports was around 16 billion US dollars, mostly copper and cobalt at high prices. The copper-cobalt belt is a powerhouse. Remember, DRC is Africa's largest copper producer and the world's largest cobalt producer. One single mine, Tenke Fungurume, produces around 20,000 plus tons of cobalt and 150,000 plus tons of copper annually, which at 2023 prices could be over 1.5 billion dollars in cobalt and 1.2 billion dollar in copper per year just from that one mine alone. The Kibali gold mine in the northeast produced 812,000 ounces of gold in 2021, which at 1,800 dollars per ounce is worth about 1.46 billion dollars. Kibali alone contributes heavily to DRC's official gold output, which is otherwise small officially, since most gold is smuggled. Formal mining operations like Kibali, Tenke, Mutanda, which is Glencore's cobalt mine, and the Kamoto Copper Company have multi-million, even billion dollar investments and employ thousands of people. They are largely mechanized and yields are well documented. However, they often enjoy tax breaks and, due to corruption, not all revenue reaches public coffers. Even so, the formal sector is profitable company and the national elite. Example, Gécamines, the state mining company, earns royalties and dividends. Politically connected individuals get contracts from here. In the best case scenario, formal mining could fund national development, but in DRC, it's been mismanaged in many instances. Still, we can say billions of dollars of minerals are legally produced each year, theoretically contributing to Congo's economy. Now, let's take a look at the informal and illegal mining sector. Alongside the formal, there's a huge artisanal and small-scale mining economy that is mostly unregulated. This include most of the 3T, that is tin, tantalum, and tungsten, and there is gold production in the Kivu and Ituri regions, which are unregulated. Quantifying it is challenging because, by nature, it's off the books, but some estimates here will help. The UN has estimated that 20 to 30 tons of gold are extracted by artisanal miners in DRC per year, of which only a tiny fraction is officially exported. In 2019, for example, officially less than 2% of that was declared. The rest of it, worth 1 to 2 billion dollars, is smuggled out. Global Witness and other NGOs have reported that DRC is losing at least 300 to 600 million dollars annually just in unpaid taxes and undeclared gold exports. For the 3Ts, at the height of conflict in late 2000s, DRC produced a significant share of global tantalum and tin. With reforms, official exports of tin from North Kivu are now measured in thousands of tons. North Kivu exported 3,900 tons of cassiterite in 2021. The price of tin hovers around 20,000 dollars per ton. So, that's about 78 million dollars value officially, but likely more is smuggled or underreported. Then we have coltan, which is tantalum ore. Official numbers are smaller, but tantalum is high value, around 150 dollars per kg in recent years. Rubaya's coltan mine, which remains under M23 control, reportedly can produce dozens of tons per month. At, say, 50 tons per month and 150 dollar per kg, that's 7.5 million dollars a month or 90 million dollar a year just from one area. Note that these are hypothetical figures. M23's tax of 800,000 dollars per month suggests the actual trade value is several times that. Because a tax might be, say, 10 to 30% of the value. So, yes, the illicit trade is in the hundreds of millions annually for each major mineral. But, how about the profits distribution? How does this work? In the formal sector, profits primarily go to the mining companies and their shareholders and to the national treasury through taxes, royalties, but due corruption siphons off a lot of that. In the informal sector, profits are distributed in a shadowy way. Warlords and smugglers capture a large share, while the miners get a meager amount for their daily survival. For example, a miner might sell a sack of coltan for 50 dollar to a trader, who sells it upward for 500 dollar, and by the time it reaches a foreign smelter, it is worth 5,000 US dollars. The price uplift largely happens once the mineral is out of the Congo. This is why companies in Dubai, Europe, or Asia and local strongmen find the trade so profitable, whereas Congolese miners remain poor. One striking stat revealed that artisanal mining supports 8 to 10 million Congolese people's livelihoods. Yet, the DRC government earns only a few million dollars in official artisanal mining taxes, meaning vast wealth is untapped or lost to illicit channels. The UN estimated armed groups in eastern DRC were making at least 185 million dollars per year from conflict minerals at one point. And as noted, conflict gold alone provides up to 600 million dollars annually to armed groups. This dwarfs any aid programs. So long as a mine can yield thousands or millions per month for whoever holds the gun, there's a strong profit incentive to continue the conflict. Now, let me show you the smuggling routes and the global supply chains used by these groups. One of the most critical links between mining and conflict in the DRC is the illicit supply chain. These are routes and mechanism by which illegally mined minerals are transported, sold, and transformed into seemingly legitimate commodities in the international market. First, it is good to note that eastern DRC is landlocked and bordered by multiple countries, making overland smuggling the norm. The main conduits are Rwanda, which is a major transit route, especially for the 3T minerals, that is tin, tantalum, and tungsten. Coltan from North Kivu often goes by road to Goma, then on trucks to Rwanda, entering either officially, with perhaps falsified documents, or through uncontrolled rural crossings. Once in Rwanda, it can be mixed with Rwandan-mined minerals and exported from Kigali as clean Rwandan ore. Rwanda's well-functioning export infrastructure, namely international flights, satisfied traders, helps launder Congolese minerals this way. Coltan and cassiterite also sometimes reach Burundi, then onward to either Rwanda or Tanzania. Also, it has been widely reported that selling minerals to Rwanda yields a better net price because Rwanda's taxes are lower than DRC's, given an incentive to smuggle out rather than export legally from the Congo. This effectively diverts trade from DRC's coffers to Rwandan traders. The M23's recent capture of territory enabled them to build a road from Rubaya mine directly into Rwanda, simplifying coltan smuggling. From Rwanda, final destinations are usually smelters in East Asia, in countries like Malaysia, Thailand for tin, China for tantalum and tungsten, or sometimes even Europe. Uganda is another country used for these smuggling operations, often the primary route for gold. Eastern DRC's gold from Ituri and South Kivu often travels by road or small aircraft into Uganda. Smuggling is then facilitated by porous borders and corruption. Despite an official ban, many vehicles still cross this place. Once in Uganda, gold is sold to brokers or directly to refineries like the African Gold Refinery. According to US authorities, over 90% of DRC's gold production is smuggled to Uganda and Rwanda, highlighting the scale of the smuggling operation. At AGR, gold from conflict areas including mines controlled by Mayi-Mayi militia is melted and recast, making its origin untraceable. Uganda then exports the refined gold legally, often to the United Arab Emirates or sometimes to Europe. Ugandan customs records and UN reports show many tons of gold leaving Uganda annually, far beyond what Uganda mines. Other gold routes include Burundi. South Kivu gold comes is smuggled by boat across the lake or by land into Burundi and then either sold there or taken to Tanzania. Sudan and South Sudan have also been minor routes for gold from northeastern DRC. The common factor is that gold moves invisibly here. It can be carried in luggage, hidden in vehicles, or even strapped to smugglers' bodies. By the time it reaches a refinery or dealer in Kampala or Bujumbura, it is extremely difficult to distinguish from clean gold. Documents can be forged or dual sets of books kept. Tanzania and Kenya are coastal countries that serve as exit points for these minerals. Conflict minerals like tin and tantalum sometimes are trucked through Burundi into Tanzania to the port of Dar es Salaam. There, they might be shipped onward to smelters in Asia. Similarly, some gold is smuggled from Congo's Kivus into Tanzania through Lake Tanganyika or via Burundi and then flown to Dubai. Kenya's port of Mombasa historically was a route for smuggled Congolese diamonds and gold during the '90s. Today, it is less central than the Rwanda-Uganda routes, but it is still occasionally used, especially from North Kivu through Uganda into Kenya. Both Tanzania and Kenya have sizable gold markets and refineries that could handle Congolese gold. Zambia and Angola are also involved in the illegal smuggling of copper and cobalt from artisanal sources in Katanga. Zambia is the main route. Cobalt ore or heterogenite, a cobalt-rich material from artisanal miners, sometimes is smuggled across the Zambia-DRC border, often with collusion of border officials, to sell to Zambian buyers. From Zambia, it is then exported as a Zambian product. Angola has been a lesser route, but during the wars, some diamonds and minerals moved through Angolan territory. Today, with cobalt so valuable, there are reports of smuggling southward into Angola or Namibia to bypass DRC controls. Across all of these routes, corruption is key. Smugglers bribe border guards, customs officers, military patrols, and even UN peacekeepers in some allegations to move shipments. A 2024 ISS report noted that poorly paid Congolese border officials at crossings to Zambia, Burundi, and Tanzania often take bribes to wave through cobalt smugglers. Similarly, militia checkpoints inside DRC often take their cut as minerals move toward frontiers. Each handoff along the chain adds a layer of distance from the original illicit source. And once out of DRC and into a neighboring state's export stream, the minerals enter the formal global market. For the 3Ts, a critical point is the smelter or refinery. These are the choke points identified by conflict mineral regulations. There are only a limited number of major smelters for tin, tantalum, and tungsten worldwide, and they in turn sell to component manufacturers. when a smelter in, say, Malaysia receives Rwandan-tagged coltan that was actually Congolese, it will produce tantalum ingots that are now virtually impossible to trace back. Downstream companies rely on paperwork provided, which has already been laundered upstream. This is how companies like Apple or Intel found themselves connected. Their suppliers buy metal from certified smelters, but if the certification schemes are corrupted, those companies inadvertently finance conflict. The Global Witness investigation showed that up to 90% of minerals entering one conflict-free scheme site were actually from unvalidated, possibly conflict sources. So, laundering occurs not just at borders, but even within supposedly responsible programs. For gold, laundering is even easier. Gold, once refined, is identical regardless of the source. Gold bought from Dubai refinery may contain metal from dozens of sources, legal or illegal. These bars are sold to banks, jewelers, or electronic firms. Dubai's role is pivotal here. It has been called out for weak oversight, essentially acting as the washing machine for dirty African gold. The UAE has pledged to tighten controls, but as of recent UN reports, conflict gold still often ends up in Dubai for purchase by dealers serving the global market. From there, it could go to a Swiss refinery for further purification or directly into jewelry manufacture. The ultimate buyers of this gold include major jewelry brands, bullion banks, and even central banks, which buy gold to hold in reserves. Most of these end users have no way of knowing their gold's bloody origins unless robust traceability is enforced, which currently it is not in the case of much artisanally mined gold. So, who ultimately benefits from this bloody economy? At the end of the chain, the ultimate beneficiaries of these minerals are widespread. The tech and electronics industry, for one, benefits from a steady supply of tantalum, tin, tungsten, and cobalt to produce consumer gadgets and appliances. Virtually every smartphone or laptop contains a few cents worth of tantalum from Congo and a few dollars worth of cobalt. This translates into billions in profit for tech companies and affordable devices for consumers, while DRC communities are left with pennies. The electric vehicle and renewable energy sector benefits from Congo's cobalt and copper. Auto giants and battery manufacturers depend on Congolese cobalt for lithium-ion batteries, and as they ramp up EV production, they benefit from large volumes of relatively cheap cobalt from DRC, often mined in substandard conditions. The world's push for green energy, ironically, has a dark underside in Congo's cobalt towns. And if we head down the gold markets, the global jewelry and bullion markets benefit from the gold. Jewelers get the gold they need for high-end products, investors get gold bars for trade and hedging. Much of this gold passes through exchanges in Dubai, Mumbai, Zurich, and London. The beauty and monetary value of gold on someone's neck or in a bank vault hides the brutal conditions under which a portion of that gold was extracted. National economies like Rwanda's and Uganda's benefit by adding value or capturing trade. Rwanda's export earnings from its minerals, Uganda's taxes, or at least the income of Uganda middlemen from the gold refining business, UAE's trade and finance sector from being the gold marketplace, all of these are ultimate beneficiaries of Congo's resources, albeit after they've been stripped away illegally. And finally, we have the consumers, who indirectly benefit from lower costs. If companies had to source only clean minerals, possibly at higher costs due to stricter labor and environmental standards, the price of electronics or jewelry might be higher. The grim reality is that the suffering of Congolese miners and the insecurity in the region have subsidized the lifestyles of consumers around the world. Every time someone buys a new smartphone or a piece of gold jewelry, there's a chance it contains a fragment of a conflict mineral from DRC. However, what the world is gradually forgetting is that there is a human cost to this trade. The conflict and illegal exploitation of minerals have had a devastating impact on the people of the DRC, especially in the east. The human cost can be seen across multiple dimensions. There are endless human rights abuses. Civilians in mining regions live under constant threat of human rights violations. Armed groups and undisciplined soldiers have committed mass killings, torture, rape, and enslavement. The prevalence of sexual violence is particularly horrifying. Tens of thousands of women and men have suffered rape, often with extreme brutality, used as a weapon to terrorize communities. Entire villages have been massacred or burned if they are suspected of siding with an enemy faction or simply to clear the area around a mine. Children have been recruited as soldiers by many groups, forced to fight or serve as porters or sex slaves. The eastern DRC conflict has been marked by horrific atrocities, leading some observers to label it a silent genocide. Though minerals are not the cause of hatred, they increase the brutality by funding these groups and giving them reasons to exert control over people. The sheer scale of abuses led the UN to have one of its largest peacekeeping forces in DRC. Yet, abuses still occur daily in some areas. Between 2017 and 2021, conflict-related killings and attacks on civilians actually increased, with over 100 armed groups operating and committing abuses. Impunity is common. Warlords rarely face justice until recently, when a few like Ntaganda and Lubanga were convicted by the ICC. But many others roam free or got integrated into the army without any form of punishment. This climate has traumatized the population. Many live in fear, having to choose between mining under armed coercion or fleeing their homes entirely. And there is also mass displacement. Eastern Congo has one of the largest internally displaced populations in the world. Over 5.6 million Congolese are internally displaced. More than 4 million of them in the eastern provinces and additionally about a million as refugees in neighboring countries. These numbers keep rising with each new clash. For instance, the recent M23 offensive displaced over 800,000 people in a matter of months. Displacement here means people abandon their farms, their homes, even schools, everything, and often end up in camps or living with host families. In these conditions, malnutrition and diseases are common. The ongoing instability, often tied to battles over resource-rich areas, means many families have been displaced multiple times over the years, never getting a chance to rebuild. A current example, in North Kivu, over 2.8 million are displaced. Many around Goma living in makeshift camps because fighting linked to M23 controlling resource areas has driven them out. Displacement also breaks social networks and can stoke ethnic tensions as people move into others' territories, sometimes causing friction. This is a humanitarian catastrophe, yet underreported globally. People of the DRC, especially children, are paying with their futures as conflict denies them education, stability, and basic services. And how about child labor and lost childhood? Many children are pulled into dangerous mining work or into armed groups. It has been estimated that in the early 2010s, some 40,000 children were working in mines in southern DRC, that is cobalt areas, and many thousands more in the east 3T and gold mines. Children as young as 7 years old have been found digging or washing minerals They suffer stunted growth, injuries, and lack of schooling. For example, around the Kolwezi cobalt mines, young boys carry heavy sacks of ore all day for a couple of dollars. In gold areas, kids inhale mercury fumes. Not only is this a gross violation of their rights, it perpetuates poverty. These kids aren't in schools, so they grow up illiterate and with few opportunities beyond the mines or militias. Child soldiers have likewise lost their childhood, forced to kill or endure abuse. The reintegration of ex-child soldiers is challenging given the trauma they've gone through. Most have to deal with diverse health crises along the way. The conflict environment and mining practices have created major health problems. In mining areas, there's often no access to clean water or medical care. Injuries on the ground go untreated or become infected. Mercury used in gold mining causes neurological damage. Many gold miners and their families have mercury in their systems from handling amalgam or from eating fish contaminated by mercury runoff. Dust and toxic metals around mines, like the fine particulates from crushing coltan or the radioactive elements that can accompany coltan or tin, causes respiratory illnesses and may contribute to birth defects. One study found more DNA damage in children near mining areas than those elsewhere and hinted at higher birth defect risk for miners' babies. Infectious diseases have flourished in war conditions with people hiding in forests or crowding in camps. Measles, cholera, and now even Ebola have taken many lives in this region. The stress and trauma also impacts mental health. PTSD and depression are widespread, but no support is forthcoming. To put things mildly, the people of the DRC have borne the burden of suffering that is immeasurable, largely because of the greed for their country's minerals. While armed actors and foreign companies make millions, ordinary Congolese have been killed, raped, uprooted, and impoverished. The human development indicators for eastern DRC read like those of a country at war, which it is, continuously impacted since the '90s. If there is any silver lining, it is that the world's awareness has increased. There are now campaigns and some programs aiming to channel the mineral wealth towards people's benefits. Example is conflict-free artisanal mining zones, donor-funded community development projects in mining However, until peace and rule of law are established, these efforts continue to face an uphill battle. And to you watching this, I think it's time you know that the true cost of our electronics and jewelry, if one factors in Congolese suffering, is astronomical and tragic. But whether or not anything can be done about this is a whole different matter.

- Prepare & deliver a research presentation | Step-by-step process: Kind: captions Language: en delivering a presentation is an important aspect of your research career if you are pursuing any form of research or writing a technical paper or you are in a degree program be it undergrad masters or phd you will have to present your research or project work in the form of a presentation so how do you prepare and deliver an impactful research presentation well let's find out hi everyone i am neha graval i am the founder of isab communications and in this video i am going to walk you through the step-by-step process that you should follow to deliver a powerful research presentation this could be for your final year project thesis defense or even a conference as well so let's begin now the first thing is organizing your presentation so even before you start preparing the slides it's important to understand what exactly is going to be the content of your presentation so similar to a research paper your research presentation also have those sections which is the introduction the materials and methods the results and discussion and the conclusion section now you can also have additional slides of acknowledgements and references towards the end while i would strongly suggest that in the beginning you add the agenda or the content slide as it really sets the expectation for your audience right in the front now coming to each individual section so for the introduction section the first thing you need to do is lay out the background of your study and after that you deep dive into the literature review basically what other researchers have achieved in that field so far once you have established the literature review then you talk about your own research so basically what is the research gap that you've identified and what is the objective of your study now if you wish to know in detail what does research objective mean what does research gap mean i've actually made a detailed video about it before which is called how to write a research paper so you can check that out next comes the materials and methods section and here you're supposed to share the methodology that you followed in order to arrive at the results now generally in a research paper what do we do we write it in text correct step by step process that we have followed i would encourage you not to do that in a presentation but try and make a pictorial representation of it in the form of an illustration basically pictures of what exactly have you done in your experiment that way it will look more attractive once you've completed the materials and methods section we now come to the results and discussion section and here you're supposed to list down your results in the form of figures graphs or tables and along with that you put few lines of comments on each result basically attaching a meaning to each and every result and that is called as discussion so my tip here again would be to not write long sentences try to keep majority of the slide with the pictures with the tables with the figures that you are adding so majority of the slides should be occupied with that just few lines of comments on each picture on each graph would be enough and finally comes the conclusion and here you can summarize your achievements novelty of your research and the scope for future work so now i hope you are very clear with the content part of your presentation basically how are you going to organize it now if you wish to know more in detail what kind of information to include in each and every section how many slides should you prepare for each topic how much time should you devote on each and every section during the delivery well for that you can join me for my presentation course it's a five hour course which covers all these aspects the link is given in the description now coming to the next part which is presentation design and preparation and this includes your template slide text and most importantly the figures and the pictures that you include coming to the template the first thing you need to finalize is the slide design and here i would highly recommend that you choose a slide design which is professional yet simple at the same time make sure it doesn't occupy a lot of slide space because you need that space to include your information you can also choose a color scheme as well for your entire presentation and stick to it coming to the slide text now one of the most common mistakes i've seen researchers make is including a lot of information on their slides guys please don't do that nobody ever reads information from the slides so whatever key points you need to include make sure you do it in the form of bullet points that's it have few sentences on the slide so that anybody who wants to understand what those pictures are for they can refer to the bullet points in fact it will even help you to deliver your presentation as these bullet points will give you a hint as to what are you supposed to speak next and finally coming to the pictures so when it comes to the pictures make sure you include high quality ones do not use watermarked pictures and whichever pictures are not original basically which are not yours and you're taking it from another sauce make sure you cite the source this is extremely important when you are delivering research presentations now if you wish to know more details of how to prepare and design your presentation well i've prepared a very good video beforehand my presentation course the link is in the description and finally coming to the last but the most important part of a research presentation and that is delivery straight away let me tell you some of the most common mistakes that researchers make while delivering a presentation first is the speed with which they talk now what happens is that most researchers have spent months if not years working on their research project and then you just give them 10 minutes to present their research so what do they do in order to crunch in as much information as possible in those 10 minutes they speak really fast and by doing that they feel they are doing justice to their research but what they don't realize is that the audience can't follow anything so what is important here is to share only that much information which can be delivered in a very slow and calm manner so that the audience can understand properly now another common mistake that most researchers make is speaking the research language when delivering the presentation now whenever we have to deliver a presentation what do we do we prepare a script we learn it up and we deliver it right now what happens with researchers is that when they are preparing the script they write it in the same way as if they write a research paper now what you need to understand is the kind of language that goes in a research paper and the kind of language that you use when delivering a research can't have the same vocabulary or the same level of complexity you need to use simpler sentences with simple vocabulary so that people can follow i'm not saying you don't use your technical terms you don't use your research jargon do that but overall sentences that you prepare it should be short and simple for people to understand i So, it has a unique interplay between demand and supply. One of the key characteristics of fine wine is its ability to age. Uh and as it age, it develops secondary and tertiary flavors. Basically, it tastes better and demand goes up. But at the same time, in the supply side, supply vintage is fixed. And then as the wines age, the supply decreases due to lost uh sorry consumption or or damage or the wines get lost. That means whenever a wine is at its peak drinking window, it's also at its scarcest. And it's this interplay between demand and supply that drives price over time. And this was the market uh being driven by COVID related factors. So first off there was an increase in the money supply. Central banks were printing money uh to ease COVID related issues through furlow payments. When you increase the money supply that also increases the price of fixed assets such as fine wine. A cohort of individuals had more disposable income. Uh they weren't spending their money on normal things like travel and holidays. So they had more money to spend on their passions like fine wine. And people were also drinking more alcohol uh over this period. Uh they weren't able to go out and socialize like they normally would. So for entertainment, alcohol consumption went up. And these factors drove the price of fine wine. But this came to an end uh around August 22. Inflation rose due to the fact there was an increase in the money supply and that caused central banks to increase interest rates. So higher interest rates are not good for the price of fine wine uh for two main reasons. First off, it reduces the risk premium from holding a liquid asset for fine wine. So there is capital uh relocation out of fine wine as an asset class, but also it increases people's propensity to save as opposed to consume, which is also not good for a consumable good. Um once we built that score, how do we know it's working? Um and this process is is called back testing. It's our historical back testing of our asset selection model aka the WS score. And what we do is we pretend it's a point in the past. So say in this example, it's 2020. We hide all of the future data from our modeling and we train our regression models on each of the predictive errors we we've identified to optimize their weightings based on historical trends. We use this trained model which only knows everything up to 2020 as if it was 2020 to make WIS scores for the full universe of investment grade wines. And then it's as simple as checking um how well our predictions did versus the actual returns over in this example the next five years. Um and we repeat this process not just for 2020 but for every year uh in our case from 20 excuse me 2009 onwards to make sure that our model was performing consistently over time consistently over market conditions um and is optimized uh for not just trends that were um temporary but is robust across time. It's very strong. The market has moved from being a UK-centric type investment to right now, worldwide. And there are more platforms cycling up all the time. In fact, the Live-ex 100, the leading industry exchange of the secondary market, has risen by around a third over the past two years. They are very, very good at putting out information as to the state of the wine market in general. And they are saying that it's extremely robust. Over the past 10 years, when it comes to luxury investments' performance, wine has trailed only cars and rare whisky, according to the Knight Frank Investment Index. But this global market is facing a global issue that could well affect investment values and prices in the longer term - climate change. The major wine regions for investment are all on the same latitude. So you start off with Napa Valley in California, on the West Coast of the United States, through to Bordeaux, through to Burgundy. And of course, these regions are getting hotter and/or colder. As our climate changes, it could make it harder to produce future vintages of the quality we're used to. Climate change is going to play a part in the wine life. That said, with wine, if there is no more Bordeaux, then the wines that are held with customers are only going to increase in value. Of course, climate change could eventually influence supply the other way by actually opening up new areas for quality wine production. The Champagne region of France, for example, is an area seen as being particularly susceptible to climate change, which is why famed champagne houses, like Pomeroy and Taittinger, are now looking to places they would never previously have considered for production. Big international players have come into the English wine scene to invest in their own vineyards, put their money where their mouth is, and recognise how special the southeast of England is. And sales for its en Primeur season, which allows collectors to buy the most recent vintage before it is bottled, were up over 40 per cent in 2021 compared with 2019. As sales grow, and with greater volumes being traded across the world, ensuring the origin and care of their wine is as it should be is becoming ever more important for collectors. The bulk of what I do is looking to check that everything that we sell is absolutely as pristine as it should be, and that it's come from the right place so that the provenance is correct, that it's been stored correctly on the way into us. In times of higher inflation, as we're seeing now, physical assets do have the potential to offer solid returns. But wine can entail higher costs than other investments due to factors like shipping and storage. It's seldom a short-term investment, and concerns about counterfeiting are also becoming more prominent. It wasn't really an issue up until about 10 years ago. Now it's something which everyone asks us about. Cases like that of Rudy Kurniawan, who in 2013 was convicted of wine fraud for selling tens of millions of fake wines, have thrown a spotlight on counterfeiting. So how big of a problem is wine fraud nowadays? The more common types of wine fraud is mass produced bottling of perfectly potable table wine with a more expensive label on it. And that's happening probably as we speak. Somewhere in the world, somebody is bottling something which is not what it says on the label. Combating the frauds takes various techniques and technologies, both old and more recent. Can you see the fluorescing lights and the fluorescing label there? Yeah. But there are two tiny little holograms in the shape of a cloverleaf. Their position moves with every vintage. Other verification systems can range from nanochips to tracking numbers and even a system that matches a unique pattern of air bubbles under the cap to an image kept on file. One relatively recent development uses blockchain technology to help gain clarity over a bottle's condition and whereabouts across the supply chain. When you've got a broken supply chain, actually, that's the best time, in my opinion, to commit fraud. Can you show me how this sort of technology would actually work in practise then? We're going to scan that code as so, and now we're going to go into temperature, humidity, and GPS location of that bottle. Systems like Chainvine won't be for everyone. But in a global survey of wineries last year, blockchain technology ranked second of all technologies they saw as having the highest priority of importance to develop. - What Is A Negociant In Rare Wine? - Luxury Life Report: Kind: captions Language: en [Music] What is a negotiant in rare wine? Have you ever wondered how some of the rarest wines make their way to collectors and connoisseurs? One key player in this world is the negotiant. So, what exactly is a negotiant in rare wine? A negotiant is a specialized wine merchant who purchases grapes, juice, or unfinished wine from various growers, often small family-owned vineyards. They then blend, bottle, market, and sell the wine under their own label. Unlike vineyard owners who manage both grape growing and wine- making, negotiants focus on the wine- making, blending, and distribution stages. This system is particularly common in French wine regions like Bordeaux and Burgundy where vineyard holdings can be small and fragmented. In the context of rare wine sourcing, negotiants play an important role by accessing rare and highquality wines from multiple small producers. Many of these producers might not have the resources to bottle or market their wine internationally. This means that high- netw worth individuals can acquire exceptional wines that might otherwise be hard to find. Negotiants also offer a broader selection and consistency. By blending wines from different vineyards or vintages, they help maintain quality and supply stability even when harvest conditions vary. This is especially beneficial for collectors who want to ensure they have a reliable source of fine wines. Additionally, negotiants provide a gateway to prestigious appellations without requiring direct contact with often private or exclusive chatau owners. In Bordeaux, for example, the negotiate system has historically acted as the primary channel for distributing classified growth wines. This means that collectors can access some of the finest wines without needing to navigate the complexities of vineyard relationships. Furthermore, negotiants enable investment and funding for vineyards by purchasing wine or grapes in advance, a practice known as n primur. This supports vineyard owners financially and helps sustain the production of luxury wines. From a luxury lifestyle management perspective, negotiants are valuable partners in sourcing rare wines. They can offer exclusive access to limited production bottles, often at prices more competitive than buying directly from the vineyard. Their knowledge in blending and aging means the wines they provide can be of exceptional quality and uniqueness. This appeals to collectors who are looking to add distinguished bottles to their sellers. In essence, a negotiant is a key figure in the rare wine market. They bridge the gap between small-scale growers and luxury wine consumers, facilitating access to rare, collectible wines while ensuring quality and availability through their merchant and wine-making activities.
